<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Bede</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<p><b>Bede</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/iː/ long 'e' in 'seed'">iː</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BEED</span></span></b></i></a></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_English_language" title="Old English language">Old English</a>: <span lang="ang" xml:lang="ang"><i>Bǣda</i> or <i>Bēda</i></span>; 672/3 – 26 May 735), also known as <b>Saint Bede</b>, <b>Venerable Bede</b>, and <b>Bede the Venerable</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la" xml:lang="la"><i>Bēda Venerābilis</i></span>), was an English, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Monk" title="Monk">monk</a> at the monastery of St. Peter and its companion monastery of St. Paul in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Northumbria" title="Kingdom of Northumbria">Kingdom of Northumbria</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a> (contemporarily <a href="/wiki/Monkwearmouth%E2%80%93Jarrow_Abbey" title="Monkwearmouth–Jarrow Abbey">Monkwearmouth–Jarrow Abbey</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tyne_and_Wear" title="Tyne and Wear">Tyne and Wear</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>). He is well known as an author and scholar, and his most famous work, <i><a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_History_of_the_English_People" title="Ecclesiastical History of the English People">Ecclesiastical History of the English People</a></i> gained him the title "The Father of <a href="/wiki/History_of_England" title="History of England">English History</a>".</p>
<p>In 1899, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_XIII" title="Pope Leo XIII">Pope Leo XIII</a> declared him a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_the_Church" title="Doctor of the Church">Doctor of the Church</a>; he is the only native of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> to achieve this designation; <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm of Canterbury</a>, also a Doctor of the Church, was originally from <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. Bede was moreover a skilled linguist and translator, and his work made the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> writings of the early <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a> much more accessible to his fellow <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>, which contributed significantly to English <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. Bede's monastery had access to an impressive library which included works by <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orosius" title="Orosius">Orosius</a>, and many others.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opera_Bedae_Venerabilis.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6168" data-file-width="4198" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Opera_Bedae_Venerabilis.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Opera_Bedae_Venerabilis.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Opera_Bedae_Venerabilis.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Opera_Bedae_Venerabilis.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Opera_Bedae_Venerabilis.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Opera_Bedae_Venerabilis.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Opera_Bedae_Venerabilis.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Opera Bedae Venerabilis</i> (1563)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Almost everything that is known of Bede's life is contained in the last chapter of his <i>Ecclesiastical History of the English People</i>, a history of the church in England. It was completed in about 731,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deconstruct5_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deconstruct5-2">[2]</a></sup> and Bede implies that he was then in his fifty-ninth year, which would give a birth date in 672 or 673.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASEEncBede_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-ASEEncBede-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xix_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xix-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[a]</a></sup> A minor source of information is the letter by his disciple Cuthbert (not to be confused with the saint, <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert" title="Cuthbert">Cuthbert</a>, who is mentioned in Bede's work) which relates Bede's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reread9_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reread9-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[b]</a></sup> Bede, in the <i>Historia</i>, gives his birthplace as "on the lands of this monastery".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HE_V_24_329_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-HE_V_24_329-11">[9]</a></sup> He is referring to the twinned monasteries of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monkwearmouth-Jarrow_Abbey" title="Monkwearmouth-Jarrow Abbey">Monkwearmouth and Jarrow</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer2004p47-48_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer2004p47-48-12">[10]</a></sup> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Sunderland,_Tyne_and_Wear" title="Sunderland, Tyne and Wear">Sunderland</a>, claimed as his birthplace; there is also a tradition that he was born at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Monkton,_Tyne_and_Wear&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Monkton, Tyne and Wear (page does not exist)">Monkton</a>, two miles from the monastery at Jarrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASEEncBede_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-ASEEncBede-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xix-xx_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xix-xx-13">[11]</a></sup> Bede says nothing of his origins, but his connections with men of noble ancestry suggest that his own family was well-to-do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World4_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-World4-14">[12]</a></sup> Bede's first abbot was <a href="/wiki/Benedict_Biscop" title="Benedict Biscop">Benedict Biscop</a>, and the names "Biscop" and "Beda" both appear in a king list of the kings of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Lindsey" title="Kingdom of Lindsey">Lindsey</a> from around 800, further suggesting that Bede came from a noble family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Beda_(name)" title="Beda (name)">Bede's name</a> reflects West Saxon <i>Bīeda</i> (Northumbrian <i>Bǣda</i>, Anglian <i>Bēda</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> It is an Anglo-Saxon short name formed on the root of <i>bēodan</i> "to bid, command".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup> The name also occurs in the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i>, s.a. 501, as <i>Bieda</i>, one of the sons of the Saxon founder of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Portsmouth#Pre-Norman" title="History of Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a>. The <i>Liber Vitae</i> of <a href="/wiki/Durham_Cathedral" title="Durham Cathedral">Durham Cathedral</a> names two priests with this name, one of whom is presumably Bede himself. Some manuscripts of the <i>Life of Cuthbert</i>, one of Bede's works, mention that Cuthbert's own priest was named Bede; it is possible that this priest is the other name listed in the <i>Liber Vitae</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reread8_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reread8-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASC14_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASC14-18">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the age of seven, Bede was sent to the monastery of Monkwearmouth by his family to be educated by Benedict Biscop and later by <a href="/wiki/Ceolfrith" title="Ceolfrith">Ceolfrith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World178_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-World178-19">[17]</a></sup> Bede does not say whether it was already intended at that point that he would be a monk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World241_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-World241-20">[18]</a></sup> It was fairly common in Ireland at this time for young boys, particularly those of noble birth, to be fostered out; the practice was also likely to have been common among the Germanic peoples in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xx_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xx-21">[19]</a></sup> Monkwearmouth's sister monastery at Jarrow was founded by Ceolfrith in 682, and Bede probably transferred to Jarrow with Ceolfrith that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer2004p47-48_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer2004p47-48-12">[10]</a></sup> The dedication stone for the church has survived to the present day; it is dated 23 April 685, and as Bede would have been required to assist with menial tasks in his day-to-day life it is possible that he helped in building the original church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xx_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xx-21">[19]</a></sup> In 686, plague broke out at Jarrow. The <i>Life of Ceolfrith</i>, written in about 710, records that only two surviving monks were capable of singing the full offices; one was Ceolfrith and the other a young boy, who according to the anonymous writer had been taught by Ceolfrith. The two managed to do the entire service of the liturgy until others could be trained. The young boy was almost certainly Bede, who would have been about 14.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World178_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-World178-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plummer_I_xii_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plummer_I_xii-22">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Bede was about 17 years old, <a href="/wiki/Adomn%C3%A1n" title="Adomnán">Adomnán</a>, the abbot of <a href="/wiki/Iona_Abbey" title="Iona Abbey">Iona Abbey</a>, visited Monkwearmouth and Jarrow. Bede would probably have met the abbot during this visit, and it may be that Adomnan sparked Bede's interest in the <a href="/wiki/Easter_controversy" title="Easter controversy">Easter dating controversy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World181_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-World181-23">[21]</a></sup> In about 692, in Bede's nineteenth year, Bede was ordained a <a href="/wiki/Deacon" title="Deacon">deacon</a> by his diocesan bishop, <a href="/wiki/John_of_Beverley" title="John of Beverley">John</a>, who was <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Hexham" title="Bishop of Hexham">bishop of Hexham</a>. The canonical age for the ordination of a deacon was 25; Bede's early ordination may mean that his abilities were considered exceptional,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xx_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xx-21">[19]</a></sup> but it is also possible that the minimum age requirement was often disregarded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World5_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-World5-24">[22]</a></sup> There might have been minor orders ranking below a deacon; but there is no record of whether Bede held any of these offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World253_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-World253-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[c]</a></sup> In Bede's thirtieth year (about 702), he became a priest, with the ordination again performed by Bishop John.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>In about 701 Bede wrote his first works, the <i>De Arte Metrica</i> and <i>De Schematibus et Tropis</i>; both were intended for use in the classroom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World5_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-World5-24">[22]</a></sup> He continued to write for the rest of his life, eventually completing over 60 books, most of which have survived. Not all his output can be easily dated, and Bede may have worked on some texts over a period of many years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World5_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-World5-24">[22]</a></sup> His last-surviving work is a letter to <a href="/wiki/Ecgbert_of_York" title="Ecgbert of York">Ecgbert of York</a>, a former student, written in 734.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World5_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-World5-24">[22]</a></sup> A 6th-century Greek and Latin manuscript of <i><a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a></i> that is believed to have been used by Bede survives and is now in the <a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library</a> at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>; it is known as the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Laudianus" title="Codex Laudianus">Codex Laudianus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World234_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-World234-26">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup> Bede may also have worked on one of the Latin bibles that were copied at Jarrow, one of which is now held by the <a href="/wiki/Laurentian_Library" title="Laurentian Library">Laurentian Library</a> in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_20a_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_20a-28">[25]</a></sup> Bede was a teacher as well as a writer;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ray_57_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ray_57-29">[26]</a></sup> he enjoyed music, and was said to be accomplished as a singer and as a reciter of poetry in the vernacular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World5_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-World5-24">[22]</a></sup> It is possible that he suffered a speech impediment, but this depends on a phrase in the introduction to his verse life of Saint Cuthbert. Translations of this phrase differ, and it is uncertain whether Bede intended to say that he was cured of a speech problem, or merely that he was inspired by the saint's works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whiting_5.E2.80.936_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whiting_5.E2.80.936-30">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FC_W_21_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FC_W_21-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[d]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bede_dictating_to_a_scribe.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="442" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Bede_dictating_to_a_scribe.JPG/220px-Bede_dictating_to_a_scribe.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Bede_dictating_to_a_scribe.JPG/330px-Bede_dictating_to_a_scribe.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Bede_dictating_to_a_scribe.JPG/440px-Bede_dictating_to_a_scribe.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bede_dictating_to_a_scribe.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stained glass at <a href="/wiki/Gloucester_Cathedral" title="Gloucester Cathedral">Gloucester Cathedral</a> depicting Bede dictating to a scribe</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 708, some monks at <a href="/wiki/Hexham_Abbey" title="Hexham Abbey">Hexham</a> accused Bede of having committed heresy in his work <i>De Temporibus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World267_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-World267-33">[29]</a></sup> The standard theological view of world history at the time was known as the <a href="/wiki/Six_Ages_of_the_World" title="Six Ages of the World">Six Ages of the World</a>; in his book, Bede calculated the age of the world for himself, rather than accepting the authority of <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">Isidore of Seville</a>, and came to the conclusion that Christ had been born 3,952 years after the creation of the world, rather than the figure of over 5,000 years that was commonly accepted by theologians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GHBBV_38_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-GHBBV_38-34">[30]</a></sup> The accusation occurred in front of the bishop of Hexham, <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid" title="Wilfrid">Wilfrid</a>, who was present at a feast when some drunken monks made the accusation. Wilfrid did not respond to the accusation, but a monk present relayed the episode to Bede, who replied within a few days to the monk, writing a letter setting forth his defence and asking that the letter also be read to Wilfrid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World267_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-World267-33">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[e]</a></sup> Bede had another brush with Wilfrid, for the historian himself says that he met Wilfrid, sometime between 706 and 709, and discussed <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelthryth" title="Æthelthryth">Æthelthryth</a>, the abbess of Ely. Wilfrid had been present at the exhumation of her body in 695, and Bede questioned the bishop about the exact circumstances of the body and asked for more details of her life, as Wilfrid had been her advisor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goffart322_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goffart322-36">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 733, Bede travelled to York to visit Ecgbert, who was then <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">bishop of York</a>. The See of York was elevated to an archbishopric in 735, and it is likely that Bede and Ecgbert discussed the proposal for the elevation during his visit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World305_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-World305-37">[32]</a></sup> Bede hoped to visit Ecgbert again in 734, but was too ill to make the journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World305_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-World305-37">[32]</a></sup> Bede also travelled to the monastery of <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">Lindisfarne</a>, and at some point visited the otherwise-unknown monastery of a monk named Wicthed, a visit that is mentioned in a letter to that monk. Because of his widespread correspondence with others throughout the British Isles, and due to the fact that many of the letters imply that Bede had met his correspondents, it is likely that Bede travelled to some other places, although nothing further about timing or locations can be guessed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reread15_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reread15-38">[33]</a></sup> It seems certain that he did not visit Rome, however, as he would have mentioned it in the autobiographical chapter of his <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColgraveMynors1969p556n_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColgraveMynors1969p556n-39">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nothhelm" title="Nothhelm">Nothhelm</a>, a correspondent of Bede's who assisted him by finding documents for him in Rome, is known to have visited Bede, though the date cannot be determined beyond the fact that it was after Nothhelm's visit to Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plummer_II_3_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plummer_II_3-40">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Except for a few visits to other monasteries, his life was spent in a round of prayer, observance of the monastic discipline and study of the Sacred Scriptures. He was considered the most learned man of his time, and wrote excellent biblical and historical books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SHMI_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-SHMI-41">[36]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bede.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="555" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Bede.jpg/220px-Bede.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Bede.jpg/330px-Bede.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Bede.jpg/440px-Bede.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bede.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bede's tomb in Durham Cathedral</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Bede died on the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Ascension" title="Feast of the Ascension">Feast of the Ascension</a>, Thursday, 26 May 735, on the floor of his cell, singing "Glory be to the Father and to the Son and to the Holy Spirit"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SHMI_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-SHMI-41">[36]</a></sup> and was buried at Jarrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> Cuthbert, a disciple of Bede's, wrote a letter to a Cuthwin (of whom nothing else is known), describing Bede's last days and his death. According to Cuthbert, Bede fell ill, "with frequent attacks of breathlessness but almost without pain", before Easter. On the Tuesday, two days before Bede died, his breathing became worse and his feet swelled. He continued to dictate to a scribe, however, and despite spending the night awake in prayer he dictated again the following day. At three o'clock, according to Cuthbert, he asked for a box of his to be brought, and distributed among the priests of the monastery "a few treasures" of his: "some pepper, and napkins, and some incense". That night he dictated a final sentence to the scribe, a boy named Wilberht, and died soon afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColgraveMynors1969pp580-587_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColgraveMynors1969pp580-587-42">[37]</a></sup> The account of Cuthbert does not make entirely clear whether Bede died before midnight or after. However, by the reckoning of Bede's time, passage from the old day to the new occurred at sunset, not midnight, and Cuthbert is clear that he died after sunset. Thus, while his box was brought at three o'clock Wednesday afternoon the 25th, by the time of the final dictation it was already Thursday the 26th."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World307_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-World307-43">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cuthbert's letter also relates a five-line poem in the vernacular that Bede composed on his deathbed, known as "Bede's Death Song". It is the most-widely copied Old English poem, and appears in 45 manuscripts, but its attribution to Bede is not certain—not all manuscripts name Bede as the author, and the ones that do are of later origin than those that do not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BEASE_59_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-BEASE_59-44">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColgraveMynors1969pp580-581n_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColgraveMynors1969pp580-581n-45">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[41]</a></sup> Bede's remains may have been transferred to Durham Cathedral in the 11th century; his tomb there was looted in 1541, but the contents were probably re-interred in the Galilee chapel at the cathedral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>One further oddity in his writings is that in one of his works, the <i>Commentary on the Seven Catholic Epistles</i>, he writes in a manner that gives the impression he was married.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reread8_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reread8-17">[15]</a></sup> The section in question is the only one in that work that is written in first-person view. Bede says: "Prayers are hindered by the conjugal duty because as often as I perform what is due to my wife I am not able to pray."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-QWard57_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-QWard57-47">[42]</a></sup> Another passage, in the <i>Commentary on Luke</i>, also mentions a wife in the first person: "Formerly I possessed a wife in the lustful passion of desire and now I possess her in honourable sanctification and true love of Christ."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-QWard57_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-QWard57-47">[42]</a></sup> The historian Benedicta Ward argues that these passages are Bede employing a rhetorical device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward57_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward57-48">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_works_by_Bede" title="List of works by Bede">List of works by Bede</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_Chronicle_Venerable_Bede.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="450" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Nuremberg_Chronicle_Venerable_Bede.jpg/170px-Nuremberg_Chronicle_Venerable_Bede.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Nuremberg_Chronicle_Venerable_Bede.jpg/255px-Nuremberg_Chronicle_Venerable_Bede.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Nuremberg_Chronicle_Venerable_Bede.jpg/340px-Nuremberg_Chronicle_Venerable_Bede.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_Chronicle_Venerable_Bede.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Depiction of the Venerable Bede (on CLVIIIv) from the <i><a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Chronicle" title="Nuremberg Chronicle">Nuremberg Chronicle</a></i>, 1493</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Bede wrote scientific, historical and theological works, reflecting the range of his writings from music and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter_(music)" title="Meter (music)">metrics</a> to exegetical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scripture" title="Scripture">Scripture</a> commentaries. He knew <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_father" title="Church father">patristic</a> literature, as well as <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucretius" title="Lucretius">Lucretius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical</a> writers. He knew some Greek.</p>
<p>Bede's scriptural commentaries employed the <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegorical</a> method of interpretation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup> and his history includes accounts of miracles, which to modern historians has seemed at odds with his critical approach to the materials in his history. Modern studies have shown the important role such concepts played in the world-view of Early Medieval scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>He dedicated his work on the Apocalypse and the <i>De Temporum Ratione</i> to the successor of Ceolfrid as abbot, Hwaetbert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World187_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-World187-51">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Bede is mainly studied as a historian now, in his time his works on grammar, chronology, and biblical studies were as important as his historical and hagiographical works. The non-historical works contributed greatly to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolingian_renaissance" title="Carolingian renaissance">Carolingian renaissance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goffart242_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goffart242-52">[47]</a></sup> He has been credited with writing a <a href="/wiki/Paenitentiale_Bedae" title="Paenitentiale Bedae">penitential</a>, though his authorship of this work is still very much disputed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historia_ecclesiastica_gentis_Anglorum"><i>Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_ecclesiastica_gentis_Anglorum" title="Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum">Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum</a></div>
<p>Bede's best-known work is the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_ecclesiastica_gentis_Anglorum" title="Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum">Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum</a></i>, or <i>An Ecclesiastical History of the English People</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_21_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_21-53">[48]</a></sup> completed in about 731. Bede was aided in writing this book by <a href="/wiki/Albinus_(abbot)" title="Albinus (abbot)">Albinus</a>, abbot of <a href="/wiki/St_Augustine%27s_Abbey" title="St Augustine's Abbey">St Augustine's Abbey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canterbury" title="Canterbury">Canterbury</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup> The first of the five books begins with some geographical background, and then sketches the history of England, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Caesar's</a> invasion in 55 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_22_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_22-55">[50]</a></sup> A brief account of Christianity in Roman Britain, including the martyrdom of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Alban" title="Saint Alban">St Alban</a>, is followed by the story of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine</a>'s mission to England in 597, which brought Christianity to the Anglo-Saxons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> The second book begins with the death of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gregory_the_Great" title="Gregory the Great">Gregory the Great</a> in 604, and follows the further progress of Christianity in Kent and the first attempts to evangelise Northumbria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_31_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_31-56">[51]</a></sup> These ended in disaster when <a href="/wiki/Penda_of_Mercia" title="Penda of Mercia">Penda</a>, the pagan king of Mercia, killed the newly Christian <a href="/wiki/Edwin_of_Northumbria" title="Edwin of Northumbria">Edwin of Northumbria</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hatfield_Chase" title="Battle of Hatfield Chase">Battle of Hatfield Chase</a> in about 632.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_31_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_31-56">[51]</a></sup> The setback was temporary, and the third book recounts the growth of Christianity in Northumbria under kings <a href="/wiki/Oswald_of_Northumbria" title="Oswald of Northumbria">Oswald of Northumbria</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oswy_of_Northumbria" title="Oswy of Northumbria">Oswy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_31.E2.80.932_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_31.E2.80.932-57">[52]</a></sup> The climax of the third book is the account of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Whitby" title="Council of Whitby">Council of Whitby</a>, traditionally seen as a major turning point in English history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitby1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitby1-58">[53]</a></sup> The fourth book begins with the consecration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodore_of_Canterbury" title="Theodore of Canterbury">Theodore</a> as <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, and recounts <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid" title="Wilfrid">Wilfrid</a>'s efforts to bring Christianity to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sussex" title="Kingdom of Sussex">kingdom of Sussex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_32_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_32-59">[54]</a></sup> The fifth book brings the story up to Bede's day, and includes an account of missionary work in Frisia, and of the conflict with the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">British church</a> over the correct dating of Easter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_32_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_32-59">[54]</a></sup> Bede wrote a preface for the work, in which he dedicates it to <a href="/wiki/Ceolwulf_of_Northumbria" title="Ceolwulf of Northumbria">Ceolwulf</a>, king of Northumbria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup> The preface mentions that Ceolwulf received an earlier draft of the book; presumably Ceolwulf knew enough Latin to understand it, and he may even have been able to read it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_22_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_22-55">[50]</a></sup> The preface makes it clear that Ceolwulf had requested the earlier copy, and Bede had asked for Ceolwulf's approval; this correspondence with the king indicates that Bede's monastery had excellent connections among the Northumbrian nobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The monastery at Wearmouth-Jarrow had an excellent library. Both Benedict Biscop and Ceolfrith had acquired books from the Continent, and in Bede's day the monastery was a renowned centre of learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BEASE_MJ_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-BEASE_MJ-61">[56]</a></sup> It has been estimated that there were about 200 books in the monastic library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BEASE_286.E2.80.937_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-BEASE_286.E2.80.937-62">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the period prior to Augustine's arrival in 597, Bede drew on earlier writers, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solinus" title="Solinus">Solinus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_25_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_25-63">[58]</a></sup> He had access to two works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius</a>: the <i><a href="/wiki/Church_History_(Eusebius)" title="Church History (Eusebius)">Historia Ecclesiastica</a></i>, and also the <i><a href="/wiki/Chronicon_(Eusebius)" title="Chronicon (Eusebius)">Chronicon</a></i>, though he had neither in the original Greek; instead he had a Latin translation of the <i>Historia</i>, by Rufinus, and Saint Jerome's translation of the <i>Chronicon</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LH_162_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-LH_162-64">[59]</a></sup> He also knew <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paulus_Orosius" title="Paulus Orosius">Orosius</a>'s <i>Adversus Paganus</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a>' <i>Historia Francorum</i>, both Christian histories,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LH_162_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-LH_162-64">[59]</a></sup> as well as the work of <a href="/wiki/Eutropius_(historian)" title="Eutropius (historian)">Eutropius</a>, a pagan historian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LH_163_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-LH_163-65">[60]</a></sup> He used <a href="/wiki/Constantius_of_Lyon" title="Constantius of Lyon">Constantius</a>'s <i>Life of Germanus</i> as a source for <a href="/wiki/Germanus_of_Auxerre" title="Germanus of Auxerre">Germanus</a>'s visits to Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-10"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_25_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_25-63">[58]</a></sup> Bede's account of the invasion of the Anglo-Saxons is drawn largely from <a href="/wiki/Gildas" title="Gildas">Gildas</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/De_Excidio_et_Conquestu_Britanniae" title="De Excidio et Conquestu Britanniae">De Excidio et Conquestu Britanniae</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BEASE_Gildas_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-BEASE_Gildas-66">[61]</a></sup> Bede would also have been familiar with more recent accounts such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eddius_Stephanus" title="Eddius Stephanus">Eddius Stephanus</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_of_Wilfrid" title="Life of Wilfrid">Life of Wilfrid</a></i>, and anonymous <i>Lives</i> of Gregory the Great and Cuthbert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_25_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_25-63">[58]</a></sup> He also drew on <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>'s <i>Antiquities</i>, and the works of <a href="/wiki/Cassiodorus" title="Cassiodorus">Cassiodorus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mey831_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mey831-67">[62]</a></sup> and there was a copy of the <i><a href="/wiki/Liber_Pontificalis" title="Liber Pontificalis">Liber Pontificalis</a></i> in Bede's monastery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mey843_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mey843-68">[63]</a></sup> Bede quotes from several classical authors, including <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">Plautus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Terence" title="Terence">Terence</a>, but he may have had access to their work via a Latin grammar rather than directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xxv_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xxv-69">[64]</a></sup> However, it is clear he was familiar with the works of <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> and with <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a></i>, and his monastery also owned copies of the works of <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_Exiguus" title="Dionysius Exiguus">Dionysius Exiguus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xxv_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xxv-69">[64]</a></sup> He probably drew his account of St. Alban from a life of that saint which has not survived. He acknowledges two other lives of saints directly; one is a life of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Fursey" title="Saint Fursey">Fursa</a>, and the other of St. <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelburh_of_Barking" title="Æthelburh of Barking">Æthelburh</a>; the latter no longer survives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plummer_I_xxiv_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plummer_I_xxiv-70">[65]</a></sup> He also had access to a life of Ceolfrith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LH_164_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-LH_164-71">[66]</a></sup> Some of Bede's material came from oral traditions, including a description of the physical appearance of <a href="/wiki/Paulinus_of_York" title="Paulinus of York">Paulinus of York</a>, who had died nearly 90 years before Bede's <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i> was written.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LH_164_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-LH_164-71">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bede also had correspondents who supplied him with material. Albinus, the abbot of the monastery in Canterbury, provided much information about the church in Kent, and with the assistance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nothelm" title="Nothelm">Nothhelm</a>, at that time a priest in London, obtained copies of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gregory_the_Great" title="Gregory the Great">Gregory the Great</a>'s correspondence from Rome relating to Augustine's mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-11"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_25_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_25-63">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BEASE_Nothhelm_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-BEASE_Nothhelm-72">[67]</a></sup> Almost all of Bede's information regarding Augustine is taken from these letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-12"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> Bede acknowledged his correspondents in the preface to the <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HE_pref_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-HE_pref-73">[68]</a></sup> he was in contact with <a href="/wiki/Daniel_of_Winchester" title="Daniel of Winchester">Daniel</a>, the Bishop of Winchester, for information about the history of the church in Wessex, and also wrote to the monastery at <a href="/wiki/Lastingham" title="Lastingham">Lastingham</a> for information about <a href="/wiki/Cedd" title="Cedd">Cedd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chad_of_Mercia" title="Chad of Mercia">Chad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HE_pref_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-HE_pref-73">[68]</a></sup> Bede also mentions an Abbot Esi as a source for the affairs of the East Anglian church, and Bishop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinebertus" title="Kinebertus">Cynibert</a> for information about Lindsey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HE_pref_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-HE_pref-73">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Walter_Goffart" title="Walter Goffart">Walter Goffart</a> argues that Bede based the structure of the <i>Historia</i> on three works, using them as the framework around which the three main sections of the work were structured. For the early part of the work, up until the Gregorian mission, Goffart feels that Bede used <a href="/wiki/Gildas" title="Gildas">Gildas</a>'s <i>De excidio</i>. The second section, detailing the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_mission" title="Gregorian mission">Gregorian mission</a> of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine of Canterbury</a> was framed on the anonymous <i>Life of Gregory the Great</i> written at Whitby. The last section, detailing events after the Gregorian mission, Goffart feels were modelled on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eddius" title="Eddius">Stephen of Ripon</a>'s <i>Life of Wilfrid</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goffart296_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goffart296-74">[69]</a></sup> Most of Bede's informants for information after Augustine's mission came from the eastern part of Britain, leaving significant gaps in the knowledge of the western areas, which were those areas likely to have a native Briton presence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deconstruct7_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deconstruct7-75">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deconstruct12_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deconstruct12-76">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Models_and_style">Models and style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Models and style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bede's stylistic models included some of the same authors from whom he drew the material for the earlier parts of his history. His introduction imitates the work of Orosius,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-13"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> and his title is an echo of Eusebius's <i><a href="/wiki/Church_History_(Eusebius)" title="Church History (Eusebius)">Historia Ecclesiastica</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASEEncBede_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-ASEEncBede-1">[1]</a></sup> Bede also followed Eusebius in taking the <i><a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a></i> as the model for the overall work: where Eusebius used the <i>Acts</i> as the theme for his description of the development of the church, Bede made it the model for his history of the Anglo-Saxon church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_26_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_26-77">[72]</a></sup> Bede quoted his sources at length in his narrative, as Eusebius had done.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-14"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> Bede also appears to have taken quotes directly from his correspondents at times. For example, he almost always uses the terms "Australes" and "Occidentales" for the South and West Saxons respectively, but in a passage in the first book he uses "Meridiani" and "Occidui" instead, as perhaps his informant had done.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-15"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> At the end of the work, Bede added a brief autobiographical note; this was an idea taken from <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a>' earlier <i>History of the Franks</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_27_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_27-78">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bede's work as a <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiographer</a>, and his detailed attention to dating, were both useful preparations for the task of writing the <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i>. His interest in <a href="/wiki/Computus" title="Computus">computus</a>, the science of calculating the date of Easter, was also useful in the account he gives of the controversy between the British and Anglo-Saxon church over the correct method of obtaining the Easter date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_21_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_21-53">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bede is described by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lapidge" title="Michael Lapidge">Michael Lapidge</a> as "without question the most accomplished Latinist produced in these islands in the Anglo-Saxon period".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup> His Latin has been praised for its clarity, but his style in the <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i> is not simple. He knew rhetoric, and often used figures of speech and rhetorical forms which cannot easily be reproduced in translation, depending as they often do on the connotations of the Latin words. However, unlike contemporaries such as <a href="/wiki/Aldhelm" title="Aldhelm">Aldhelm</a>, whose Latin is full of difficulties, Bede's own text is easy to read.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xxxvii_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xxxvii-80">[75]</a></sup> In the words of Charles Plummer, one of the best-known editors of the <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i>, Bede's Latin is "clear and limpid ... it is very seldom that we have to pause to think of the meaning of a sentence ... Alcuin rightly praises Bede for his unpretending style."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plummer_I_liii_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plummer_I_liii-81">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intent">Intent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Intent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bede's primary intention in writing the <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i> was to show the growth of the united church throughout England. The native Britons, whose Christian church survived the departure of the Romans, earn Bede's ire for refusing to help convert the Saxons; by the end of the <i>Historia</i> the English, and their Church, are dominant over the Britons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xxx_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xxx-82">[77]</a></sup> This goal, of showing the movement towards unity, explains Bede's animosity towards the British method of calculating Easter: much of the <i>Historia</i> is devoted to a history of the dispute, including the final resolution at the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Whitby" title="Synod of Whitby">Synod of Whitby</a> in 664.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_27_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_27-78">[73]</a></sup> Bede is also concerned to show the unity of the English, despite the disparate kingdoms that still existed when he was writing. He also wants to instruct the reader by spiritual example, and to entertain, and to the latter end he adds stories about many of the places and people about which he wrote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xxx_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xxx-82">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>N.J. Higham argues that Bede designed his work to promote his reform agenda to Ceolwulf, the Northumbrian king. Bede painted a highly optimistic picture of the current situation in the Church, as opposed to the more pessimistic picture found in his private letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bede's extensive use of miracles can prove difficult for readers who consider him a more or less reliable historian, but do not accept the possibility of miracles. Yet both reflect an inseparable integrity and regard for accuracy and truth, expressed in terms both of historical events and of a tradition of Christian faith that continues to the present day. Bede, like Gregory the Great whom Bede quotes on the subject in the <i>Historia</i>, felt that faith brought about by miracles was a stepping stone to a higher, truer faith, and that as a result miracles had their place in a work designed to instruct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xxxiv_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xxxiv-84">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Omissions_and_biases">Omissions and biases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Omissions and biases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bede is somewhat reticent about the career of <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid" title="Wilfrid">Wilfrid</a>, a contemporary and one of the most prominent clerics of his day. This may be because Wilfrid's opulent lifestyle was uncongenial to Bede's monastic mind; it may also be that the events of Wilfrid's life, divisive and controversial as they were, simply did not fit with Bede's theme of the progression to a unified and harmonious church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer_31_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer_31-56">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bede's account of the early migrations of the Angles and Saxons to England omits any mention of a movement of those peoples across the channel from Britain to Brittany described by <a href="/wiki/Procopius" title="Procopius">Procopius</a>, who was writing in the sixth century. <a href="/wiki/Frank_Stenton" title="Frank Stenton">Frank Stenton</a> describes this omission as "a scholar's dislike of the indefinite"; traditional material that could not be dated or used for Bede's didactic purposes had no interest for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stenton_8.E2.80.939_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stenton_8.E2.80.939-85">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bede was a Northumbrian, and this tinged his work with a local bias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JMWH_xxxi_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-JMWH_xxxi-86">[81]</a></sup> The sources he had access to gave him less information about the west of England than for other areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCOB_119_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-TCOB_119-87">[82]</a></sup> He says relatively little about the achievements of Mercia and Wessex, omitting, for example, any mention of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boniface" title="Boniface">Boniface</a>, a West Saxon missionary to the continent of some renown and of whom Bede had almost certainly heard, though Bede does discuss Northumbrian missionaries to the continent. He also is parsimonious in his praise for <a href="/wiki/Aldhelm" title="Aldhelm">Aldhelm</a>, a West Saxon who had done much to convert the native Britons to the Roman form of Christianity. He lists seven kings of the Anglo-Saxons whom he regards as having held <i>imperium</i>, or overlordship; only one king of Wessex, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceawlin" title="Ceawlin">Ceawlin</a>, is listed, and none from Mercia, though elsewhere he acknowledges the secular power several of the Mercians held.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCOB_21.E2.80.932_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-TCOB_21.E2.80.932-88">[83]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Robin_Fleming" title="Robin Fleming">Robin Fleming</a> states that he was so hostile to Mercia because Northumbria had been diminished by Mercian power that he consulted no Mercian informants and included no stories about its saints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bede relates the story of Augustine's mission from Rome, and tells how the British clergy refused to assist Augustine in the conversion of the Anglo-Saxons. This, combined with Gildas's negative assessment of the British church at the time of the Anglo-Saxon invasions, led Bede to a very critical view of the native church. However, Bede ignores the fact that at the time of Augustine's mission, the history between the two was one of warfare and conquest, which, in the words of <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Yorke" title="Barbara Yorke">Barbara Yorke</a>, would have naturally "curbed any missionary impulses towards the Anglo-Saxons from the British clergy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCOB_118_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-TCOB_118-90">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_Anno_Domini">Use of <i>Anno Domini</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Use of Anno Domini">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the time Bede wrote the <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i>, there were two common ways of referring to dates. One was to use <a href="/wiki/Indiction" title="Indiction">indictions</a>, which were 15-year cycles, counting from 312 AD. There were three different varieties of indiction, each starting on a different day of the year. The other approach was to use regnal years—the reigning Roman emperor, for example, or the ruler of whichever kingdom was under discussion. This meant that in discussing conflicts between kingdoms, the date would have to be given in the regnal years of all the kings involved. Bede used both these approaches on occasion, but adopted a third method as his main approach to dating: the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anno_domini" title="Anno domini">anno domini</a></i> method invented by <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_Exiguus" title="Dionysius Exiguus">Dionysius Exiguus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xviii_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xviii-91">[86]</a></sup> Although Bede did not invent this method, his adoption of it, and his promulgation of it in <i>De Temporum Ratione</i>, his work on chronology, is the main reason why it is now so widely used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xviii_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xviii-91">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stenton_186_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stenton_186-92">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assessment">Assessment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Assessment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i> was copied often in the Middle Ages, and about 160 manuscripts containing it survive. About half of those are located on the European continent, rather than on the British Isles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Companion4_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Companion4-93">[88]</a></sup> Most of the 8th- and 9th-century texts of Bede's <i>Historia</i> come from the northern parts of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolingian" title="Carolingian">Carolingian</a> Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reread21_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reread21-94">[89]</a></sup> This total does not include manuscripts with only a part of the work, of which another 100 or so survive. It was printed for the first time between 1474 and 1482, probably at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strasbourg,_France" title="Strasbourg, France">Strasbourg, France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Companion4_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Companion4-93">[88]</a></sup> Modern historians have studied the <i>Historia</i> extensively, and a number of editions have been produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nar236_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nar236-95">[90]</a></sup> For many years, early Anglo-Saxon history was essentially a retelling of the <i>Historia</i>, but recent scholarship has focused as much on what Bede did not write as what he did. The belief that the <i>Historia</i> was the culmination of Bede's works, the aim of all his scholarship, a belief common among historians in the past, is no longer accepted by most scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nar238_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nar238-96">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Modern historians and editors of Bede have been lavish in their praise of his achievement in the <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i>. Stenton regarded it as one of the "small class of books which transcend all but the most fundamental conditions of time and place", and regarded its quality as dependent on Bede's "astonishing power of co-ordinating the fragments of information which came to him through tradition, the relation of friends, or documentary evidence ... In an age where little was attempted beyond the registration of fact, he had reached the conception of history."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stenton_187_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stenton_187-97">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Wormald" title="Patrick Wormald">Patrick Wormald</a> described him as "the first and greatest of England's historians".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worm29_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Worm29-98">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i> has given Bede a high reputation, but his concerns were different from those of a modern writer of history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-16"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> His focus on the history of the organisation of the English church, and on heresies and the efforts made to root them out, led him to exclude the secular history of kings and kingdoms except where a moral lesson could be drawn or where they illuminated events in the church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-17"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> Besides the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i>, the medieval writers <a href="/wiki/William_of_Malmesbury" title="William of Malmesbury">William of Malmesbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_of_Huntingdon" title="Henry of Huntingdon">Henry of Huntingdon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_of_Monmouth" title="Geoffrey of Monmouth">Geoffrey of Monmouth</a> used his works as sources and inspirations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reread27_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reread27-99">[94]</a></sup> Early modern writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Polydore_Vergil" title="Polydore Vergil">Polydore Vergil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Parker" title="Matthew Parker">Matthew Parker</a>, the Elizabethan Archbishop of Canterbury, also utilised the <i>Historia</i>, and his works were used by both Protestant and Catholic sides in the Wars of Religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reread33_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reread33-100">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some historians have questioned the reliability of some of Bede's accounts. One historian, Charlotte Behr, thinks that the <i>Historia's</i> account of the arrival of the Germanic invaders in Kent should not be considered to relate what actually happened, but rather relates myths that were current in Kent during Bede's time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behr_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Behr-101">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is likely that Bede's work, because it was so widely copied, discouraged others from writing histories and may even have led to the disappearance of manuscripts containing older historical works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plummer_I_xlvii_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plummer_I_xlvii-102">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_historical_works">Other historical works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other historical works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Athelstan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1898" data-file-width="1347" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Athelstan.jpg/170px-Athelstan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Athelstan.jpg/255px-Athelstan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Athelstan.jpg/340px-Athelstan.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Athelstan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A page from a copy of Bede's <i>Lives of St. Cuthbert</i>, showing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athelstan_of_England" title="Athelstan of England">King Athelstan</a> presenting the work to the saint. This manuscript was given to St. Cuthbert's shrine in 934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIHBM_42_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-OIHBM_42-103">[98]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronicles">Chronicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Chronicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As Chapter 66 of his <i>On the Reckoning of Time</i>, in 725 Bede wrote the <i>Greater Chronicle</i> (<i>chronica maiora</i>), which sometimes circulated as a separate work. For recent events the <i>Chronicle</i>, like his <i>Ecclesiastical History</i>, relied upon Gildas, upon a version of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liber_pontificalis" title="Liber pontificalis">Liber pontificalis</a> current at least to the papacy of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Sergius_I" title="Pope Sergius I">Pope Sergius I</a> (687–701), and other sources. For earlier events he drew on <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a>'s <i>Chronikoi Kanones.</i> The dating of events in the <i>Chronicle</i> is inconsistent with his other works, using the era of creation, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anno_mundi" title="Anno mundi">anno mundi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lives">Lives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Lives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>His other historical works included lives of the abbots of <a href="/wiki/Jarrow#Wearmouth-Jarrow_Priory" title="Jarrow">Wearmouth and Jarrow</a>, as well as verse and prose lives of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuthbert_of_Lindisfarne" title="Cuthbert of Lindisfarne">Saint Cuthbert of Lindisfarne</a>, an adaptation of <a href="/wiki/Paulinus_of_Nola" title="Paulinus of Nola">Paulinus of Nola</a>'s <i>Life of St Felix</i>, and a translation of the Greek <i>Passion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Anastasius" title="Saint Anastasius">St Anastasius</a></i>. He also created a listing of saints, the <i>Martyrology</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goffart245_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goffart245-105">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theological_works">Theological works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Theological works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In his own time, Bede was as well known for his biblical commentaries and exegetical, as well as other theological works. The majority of his writings were of this type, and covered the Old Testament and the New Testament. Most survived the Middle Ages, but a few were lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BedeVen42_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-BedeVen42-106">[101]</a></sup> It was for his theological writings that he earned the title of <i>Doctor Anglorum</i>, and why he was made a saint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theo_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theo-107">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bede synthesised and transmitted the learning from his predecessors, as well as made careful, judicious innovation in knowledge (such as recalculating the age of the earth—for which he was censured before surviving the heresy accusations and eventually having his views championed by Archbishop Ussher in the sixteenth century—see below) that had theological implications. In order to do this, he learned Greek, and attempted to learn Hebrew. He spent time reading and rereading both the Old and the New Testaments. He mentions that he studied from a text of <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>, which itself was from the Hebrew text. He also studied both the Latin and the Greek Fathers of the Church. In the monastic library at Jarrow were a number of books by theologians, including works by <a href="/wiki/Basil_of_Caesarea" title="Basil of Caesarea">Basil</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Cassian" title="John Cassian">Cassian</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Chrysostom" title="John Chrysostom">John Chrysostom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">Isidore of Seville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nazianzus" title="Gregory of Nazianzus">Gregory of Nazianzus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Pope Gregory I</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambrose_of_Milan" title="Ambrose of Milan">Ambrose of Milan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cassiodorus" title="Cassiodorus">Cassiodorus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cyprian" title="Cyprian">Cyprian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xxv_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xxv-69">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theo_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Theo-107">[102]</a></sup> He used these, in conjunction with the Biblical texts themselves, to write his commentaries and other theological works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theo_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-Theo-107">[102]</a></sup> He had a Latin translation by <a href="/wiki/Evagrius_of_Antioch" title="Evagrius of Antioch">Evagrius</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athanasius" title="Athanasius">Athanasius</a>'s <i>Life of Antony</i>, and a copy of <a href="/wiki/Sulpicius_Severus" title="Sulpicius Severus">Sulpicius Severus</a>' <i>Life of St. Martin</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xxv_69-3"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xxv-69">[64]</a></sup> He also used lesser known writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Fulgentius_of_Ruspe" title="Fulgentius of Ruspe">Fulgentius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julian_of_Eclanum" title="Julian of Eclanum">Julian of Eclanum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyconius" title="Tyconius">Tyconius</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prosper_of_Aquitaine" title="Prosper of Aquitaine">Prosper of Aquitaine</a>. Bede was the first to refer to Jerome, Augustine, Pope Gregory and Ambrose as the four Latin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fathers_of_the_Church" title="Fathers of the Church">Fathers of the Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward44_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward44-108">[103]</a></sup> It is clear from Bede's own comments that he felt his job was to explain to his students and readers the theology and thoughts of the Church Fathers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mey827_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mey827-109">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bede also wrote homilies, works written to explain theology used in worship services. Bede wrote homilies not only on the major Christian seasons such as <a href="/wiki/Advent" title="Advent">Advent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a>, or Easter, but on other subjects such as anniversaries of significant events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theo_107-3"><a href="#cite_note-Theo-107">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Both types of Bede's theological works circulated widely in the Middle Ages. A number of his biblical commentaries were incorporated into the <i><a href="/wiki/Glossa_Ordinaria" title="Glossa Ordinaria">Glossa Ordinaria</a></i>, an 11th-century collection of biblical commentaries. Some of Bede's homilies were collected by <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Deacon" title="Paul the Deacon">Paul the Deacon</a>, and they were used in that form in the <a href="/wiki/Liturgy_of_the_Hours" title="Liturgy of the Hours">Monastic Office</a>. <a href="/wiki/Saint_Boniface" title="Saint Boniface">Saint Boniface</a> used Bede's homilies in his missionary efforts on the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theo_107-4"><a href="#cite_note-Theo-107">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bede sometimes included in his theological books an acknowledgement of the predecessors on whose works he drew. In two cases he left instructions that his marginal notes, which gave the details of his sources, should be preserved by the copyist, and he may have originally added marginal comments about his sources to others of his works. Where he does not specify, it is still possible to identify books to which he must have had access by quotations that he uses. A full catalogue of the library available to Bede in the monastery cannot be reconstructed, but it is possible to tell, for example, that Bede was very familiar with the works of Virgil. There is little evidence that he had access to any other of the pagan Latin writers—he quotes many of these writers but the quotes are almost all to be found in the Latin grammars that were common in his day, one or more of which would certainly have been at the monastery. Another difficulty is that manuscripts of early writers were often incomplete: it is apparent that Bede had access to Pliny's <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Encyclopedia</a></i>, for example, but it seems that the version he had was missing book xviii, as he would almost certainly have quoted from it in his <i>De temporum ratione</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BLTW_Laistner_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-BLTW_Laistner-110">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[f]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Works_on_the_Old_Testament">Works on the Old Testament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Works on the Old Testament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The works dealing with the Old Testament included <i>Commentary on Samuel</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward67_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward67-113">[107]</a></sup> <i>Commentary on Genesis</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward68_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward68-114">[108]</a></sup> <i>Commentaries on Ezra and Nehemiah</i>, <i>On the Temple</i>, <i>On the Tabernacle</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward72_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward72-115">[109]</a></sup> <i>Commentaries on Tobit</i>, <i>Commentaries on Proverbs</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Obermair45_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Obermair45-116">[110]</a></sup> <i>Commentaries on the Song of Songs</i>, <i>Commentaries on the Canticle of Habakkuk</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward74_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward74-117">[111]</a></sup> The works on Ezra, the Tabernacle and the Temple were especially influenced by Gregory the Great's writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory80_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory80-118">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Works_on_the_New_Testament">Works on the New Testament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Works on the New Testament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bede's works included <i>Commentary on Revelation</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward51_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward51-119">[113]</a></sup> <i>Commentary on the Catholic Epistles</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward56_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward56-120">[114]</a></sup> <i>Commentary on Acts</i>, <i>Reconsideration on the Books of Acts</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward58_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward58-121">[115]</a></sup> <i>On the Gospel of Mark</i>, <i>On the Gospel of Luke</i>, and <i>Homilies on the Gospels</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward60_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward60-122">[116]</a></sup> At the time of his death he was working on a translation of the Gospel of St. John into English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loyn270_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loyn270-123">[117]</a></sup> He did this for the last 40 days of his life. When the last passage had been translated he said: "All is finished."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SHMI_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-SHMI-41">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works_on_historical_and_astronomical_chronology">Works on historical and astronomical chronology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Works on historical and astronomical chronology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beda_-_De_natura_rerum,_1529_-_4784142.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4550" data-file-width="2900" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Beda_-_De_natura_rerum%2C_1529_-_4784142.tif/lossy-page1-170px-Beda_-_De_natura_rerum%2C_1529_-_4784142.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Beda_-_De_natura_rerum%2C_1529_-_4784142.tif/lossy-page1-255px-Beda_-_De_natura_rerum%2C_1529_-_4784142.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Beda_-_De_natura_rerum%2C_1529_-_4784142.tif/lossy-page1-340px-Beda_-_De_natura_rerum%2C_1529_-_4784142.tif.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beda_-_De_natura_rerum,_1529_-_4784142.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>De natura rerum</i>, 1529</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>De temporibus</i>, or <i>On Time</i>, written in about 703, provides an introduction to the principles of Easter <a href="/wiki/Computus#History" title="Computus">computus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BedeVen37_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-BedeVen37-124">[118]</a></sup> This was based on parts of <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">Isidore of Seville</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Etymologiae" title="Etymologiae">Etymologies</a></i>, and Bede also included a chronology of the world which was derived from Eusebius, with some revisions based on Jerome's translation of the bible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-18"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> In about 723,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-19"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> Bede wrote a longer work on the same subject, <i>On the Reckoning of Time</i>, which was influential throughout the Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BedeVen38_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-BedeVen38-125">[119]</a></sup> He also wrote several shorter letters and essays discussing specific aspects of computus.</p>
<p><i>On the Reckoning of Time</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Reckoning_of_Time" title="The Reckoning of Time">De temporum ratione</a></i>) included an introduction to the traditional ancient and medieval view of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">cosmos</a>, including an explanation of how the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spherical_earth" title="Spherical earth">spherical earth</a> influenced the changing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Length_of_day" title="Length of day">length of daylight</a>, of how the <a href="/wiki/Season" title="Season">seasonal</a> motion of the Sun and Moon influenced the changing appearance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Moon" title="New Moon">New Moon</a> at evening twilight, and a quantitative relation between the changes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tides" title="Tides">tides</a> at a given place and the daily motion of the moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bede2004pp82-85_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bede2004pp82-85-126">[120]</a></sup> Since the focus of his book was the <a href="/wiki/Computus" title="Computus">computus</a>, Bede gave instructions for <a href="/wiki/Easter_controversy#Third_phase" title="Easter controversy">computing the date of Easter</a> and the related time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paschal_Full_Moon" title="Paschal Full Moon">Easter Full Moon</a>, for calculating the motion of the Sun and Moon through the <a href="/wiki/Zodiac" title="Zodiac">zodiac</a>, and for many other calculations related to the calendar. He gives some information about the months of the Anglo-Saxon calendar in chapter XV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bede2004pp53-4_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bede2004pp53-4-127">[121]</a></sup> Any codex of <a href="/wiki/Beda_Venerabilis%27_Easter_cycle" title="Beda Venerabilis' Easter cycle">Bede's Easter cycle</a> is normally found together with a codex of his "De Temporum Ratione".</p>
<p>For calendric purposes, Bede made a new calculation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dating_Creation" title="Dating Creation">age of the world</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Genesis_creation_narrative" title="Genesis creation narrative">creation</a>, which he dated as 3952 BC.
