Due to his innovations in computing the age of the world, he was accused of heresy at the table of Bishop <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid" title="Wilfrid">Wilfrid</a>, his chronology being contrary to accepted calculations. Once informed of the accusations of these "lewd rustics," Bede refuted them in his Letter to Plegwin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bede2004ppxxx_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bede2004ppxxx-128">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to these works on astronomical timekeeping, he also wrote <i>De natura rerum</i>, or <i>On the Nature of Things</i>, modelled in part after the work of the same title by <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">Isidore of Seville</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BedeVen36_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-BedeVen36-129">[123]</a></sup> His works were so influential that late in the 9th century <a href="/wiki/Notker_the_Stammerer" title="Notker the Stammerer">Notker the Stammerer</a>, a monk of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbey_of_St._Gall" title="Abbey of St. Gall">Monastery of St. Gall</a> in Switzerland, wrote that "God, the orderer of natures, who raised the Sun from the East on the fourth day of Creation, in the sixth day of the world has made Bede rise from the West as a new Sun to illuminate the whole Earth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bede2004plxxxv_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bede2004plxxxv-130">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_works">Educational works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Educational works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bede wrote some works designed to help teach grammar in the abbey school. One of these was his <i>De arte metrica</i>, a discussion of the composition of Latin verse, drawing on previous grammarians work. It was based on Donatus' <i>De pedibus</i> and <a href="/wiki/Maurus_Servius_Honoratus" title="Maurus Servius Honoratus">Servius</a>' <i>De finalibus</i>, and used examples from Christian poets as well as Virgil. It became a standard text for the teaching of Latin verse during the next few centuries. Bede dedicated this work to Cuthbert, apparently a student, for he is named "beloved son" in the dedication, and Bede says "I have laboured to educate you in divine letters and ecclesiastical statutes"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BedeVen31_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-BedeVen31-131">[125]</a></sup> Another textbook of Bede's is the <i>De orthographia</i>, a work on <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a>, designed to help a medieval reader of Latin with unfamiliar abbreviations and words from classical Latin works. Although it could serve as a textbook, it appears to have been mainly intended as a reference work. The exact date of composition for both of these works is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BedeVen35_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-BedeVen35-132">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another educational work is <i>De schematibus et tropis sacrae scripturae</i>, which discusses the Bible's use of rhetoric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-20"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> Bede was familiar with pagan authors such as Virgil, but it was not considered appropriate to teach biblical grammar from such texts, and in <i>De schematibus ...</i> Bede argues for the superiority of Christian texts in understanding Christian literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-21"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_xxvi_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-CM_xxvi-133">[127]</a></sup> Similarly, his text on poetic metre uses only Christian poetry for examples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-22"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vernacular_poetry">Vernacular poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Vernacular poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to his disciple Cuthbert, Bede was also <i>doctus in nostris carminibus</i> ("learned in our songs"). Cuthbert's letter on Bede's death, the <i>Epistola Cuthberti de obitu Bedae</i>, moreover, commonly is understood to indicate that Bede also composed a five line vernacular poem known to modern scholars as <i>Bede's Death Song</i></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>And he used to repeat that sentence from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" title="Paul of Tarsus">St. Paul</a> "It is a fearful thing to fall into the hands of the living God," and many other verses of Scripture, urging us thereby to awake from the slumber of the soul by thinking in good time of our last hour. And in our own language,—for he was familiar with English poetry,—speaking of the soul's dread departure from the body:</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<table>
<tr role="presentation" style="vertical-align: top;">
<td>
<div class="poem" style="font-style:italic;text-align:left">
<p><span lang="ang" xml:lang="ang">Fore ðæm nedfere nænig wiorðe<br/>
ðonc snottora ðon him ðearf siæ<br/>
to ymbhycgenne ær his hinionge<br/>
hwæt his gastæ godes oððe yfles<br/>
æfter deað dæge doemed wiorðe.</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColgraveMynors1969pp580-3_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColgraveMynors1969pp580-3-134">[128]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</td>
<td><span style="padding-left:1em;"> </span></td>
<td>
<div class="poem">
<p>Facing that enforced journey, no man can be<br/>
More prudent than he has good call to be,<br/>
If he consider, before his going hence,<br/>
What for his spirit of good hap or of evil<br/>
After his day of death shall be determined.</p>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="display: none;">
<td></td>
<td></td>
<td></td>
</tr>
</table>
</dd>
</dl>
</blockquote>
<p>As Opland notes, however, it is not entirely clear that Cuthbert is attributing this text to Bede: most manuscripts of the letter do not use a <a href="/wiki/Finite_verb" title="Finite verb">finite verb</a> to describe Bede's presentation of the song, and the theme was relatively common in Old English and Anglo-Latin literature. The fact that Cuthbert's description places the performance of the Old English poem in the context of a series of quoted passages from Sacred Scripture, indeed, might be taken as evidence simply that Bede also cited analogous vernacular texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Opland140_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Opland140-135">[129]</a></sup> On the other hand, the inclusion of the Old English text of the poem in Cuthbert's Latin letter, the observation that Bede "was learned in our song," and the fact that Bede composed a Latin poem on the same subject all point to the possibility of his having written it. By citing the poem directly, Cuthbert seems to imply that its particular wording was somehow important, either since it was a vernacular poem endorsed by a scholar who evidently frowned upon secular entertainment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mccready14_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mccready14-136">[130]</a></sup> or because it is a direct quotation of Bede's last original composition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Opland14_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Opland14-137">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Veneration">Veneration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Veneration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Depiction_of_St._Bede_the_Venerable_(at_St._Bede%27s_school,_Chennai)_-_Image_has_been_cropped_for_better_presentation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1310" data-file-width="896" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Depiction_of_St._Bede_the_Venerable_%28at_St._Bede%27s_school%2C_Chennai%29_-_Image_has_been_cropped_for_better_presentation.jpg/170px-Depiction_of_St._Bede_the_Venerable_%28at_St._Bede%27s_school%2C_Chennai%29_-_Image_has_been_cropped_for_better_presentation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Depiction_of_St._Bede_the_Venerable_%28at_St._Bede%27s_school%2C_Chennai%29_-_Image_has_been_cropped_for_better_presentation.jpg/255px-Depiction_of_St._Bede_the_Venerable_%28at_St._Bede%27s_school%2C_Chennai%29_-_Image_has_been_cropped_for_better_presentation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Depiction_of_St._Bede_the_Venerable_%28at_St._Bede%27s_school%2C_Chennai%29_-_Image_has_been_cropped_for_better_presentation.jpg/340px-Depiction_of_St._Bede_the_Venerable_%28at_St._Bede%27s_school%2C_Chennai%29_-_Image_has_been_cropped_for_better_presentation.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Depiction_of_St._Bede_the_Venerable_(at_St._Bede%27s_school,_Chennai)_-_Image_has_been_cropped_for_better_presentation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bede depicted at St. Bede's school, <a href="/wiki/Chennai" title="Chennai">Chennai</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is no evidence for cult being paid to Bede in England in the 8th century. One reason for this may be that he died on the feast day of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine of Canterbury</a>. Later, when he was <a href="/wiki/Veneration" title="Veneration">venerated</a> in England, he was either commemorated after Augustine on 26 May, or his feast was moved to 27 May. However, he was venerated outside England, mainly through the efforts of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Boniface" title="Saint Boniface">Boniface</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcuin" title="Alcuin">Alcuin</a>, both of whom promoted the cult on the Continent. Boniface wrote repeatedly back to England during his missionary efforts, requesting copies of Bede's theological works. Alcuin, who was taught at the school set up in York by Bede's pupil <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecgbert_(bishop)" title="Ecgbert (bishop)">Egbert</a>, praised Bede as an example for monks to follow and was instrumental in disseminating Bede's works to all of Alcuin's friends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward136_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward136-138">[132]</a></sup> Bede's cult became prominent in England during the 10th-century revival of monasticism, and by the 14th century had spread to many of the cathedrals of England. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wulfstan_(Bishop_of_Worcester)" title="Wulfstan (Bishop of Worcester)">Wulfstan, Bishop of Worcester</a> (c. 1008–1095) was a particular devotee of Bede's, dedicating a church to him in 1062, which was Wulfstan's first undertaking after his consecration as bishop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward139_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward139-139">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>His body was '<a href="/wiki/Translation_(relic)" title="Translation (relic)">translated</a>' (the ecclesiastical term for relocation of relics) from Jarrow to Durham Cathedral around 1020, where it was placed in the same tomb with Saint <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuthbert_of_Lindisfarne" title="Cuthbert of Lindisfarne">Cuthbert of Lindisfarne</a>. Later Bede's remains were moved to a shrine in the Galilee Chapel at <a href="/wiki/Durham_Cathedral" title="Durham Cathedral">Durham Cathedral</a> in 1370. The shrine was destroyed during the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a>, but the bones were reburied in the chapel. In 1831 the bones were dug up and then reburied in a new tomb, which is still there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Companion4pic_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Companion4pic-140">[134]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> were claimed by <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury_Abbey" title="Glastonbury Abbey">Glastonbury</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmer2004p47-48_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Farmer2004p47-48-12">[10]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Fulda" title="Fulda">Fulda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reread24_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reread24-141">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>His scholarship and importance to Catholicism were recognised in 1899 when he was declared a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_the_Church" title="Doctor of the Church">Doctor of the Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_4-23"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-4">[4]</a></sup> He is the only Englishman named a Doctor of the Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SHMI_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-SHMI-41">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Companion4_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Companion4-93">[88]</a></sup> He is also the only Englishman in <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Paradiso_(Dante)" title="Paradiso (Dante)">Paradise</a></i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Paradiso_(Dante)" title="Paradiso (Dante)">Paradiso</a></i> X.130), mentioned among theologians and doctors of the church in the same canto as <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">Isidore of Seville</a> and the Scot <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_of_St._Victor" title="Richard of St. Victor">Richard of St. Victor</a>.</p>
<p>His feast day was included in the <a href="/wiki/General_Roman_Calendar" title="General Roman Calendar">General Roman Calendar</a> in 1899, for celebration on 27 May rather than on his date of death, 26 May, which was then the feast day of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_VII" title="Pope Gregory VII">Pope Saint Gregory VII</a>. He is venerated in both the Anglican and Roman Catholic Church, with a feast day of 25 May,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Companion4_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-Companion4-93">[88]</a></sup> and in the Eastern Orthodox Church, with a feast day on 27 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bede became known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venerable" title="Venerable">Venerable</a> Bede</i> (Lat.: Beda Venerabilis) by the 9th century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Companion3_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Companion3-143">[137]</a></sup> because of his holiness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SHMI_41-4"><a href="#cite_note-SHMI-41">[36]</a></sup> but this was not linked to consideration for <a href="/wiki/Canonization" title="Canonization">sainthood</a> by the Roman Catholic Church. According to a legend the epithet was miraculously supplied by angels, thus completing his unfinished epitaph.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[138]</a></sup> It is first utilised in connection with Bede in the 9th century, where Bede was grouped with others who were called "venerable" at two ecclesiastical councils held at Aachen in 816 and 836. <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Deacon" title="Paul the Deacon">Paul the Deacon</a> then referred to him as venerable consistently. By the 11th and 12th century, it had become commonplace. However, there are no descriptions of Bede by that term right after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reread9_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Reread9-9">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_legacy">Modern legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Modern legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bede's reputation as a historian, based mostly on the <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i>, remains strong;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stenton_187_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stenton_187-97">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worm29_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Worm29-98">[93]</a></sup> historian <a href="/wiki/Walter_Goffart" title="Walter Goffart">Walter Goffart</a> says of Bede that he "holds a privileged and unrivalled place among first historians of Christian Europe".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nar236_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nar236-95">[90]</a></sup> His life and work have been celebrated with the annual Jarrow Lecture, held at St. Paul's Church, Jarrow, since 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bede's words are "Ex quo tempore accepti presbyteratus usque ad annum aetatis meae LVIIII ..."; which means "From the time I became a priest until the fifty-ninth year of my life I have made it my business ... to make brief extracts from the works of the venerable fathers on the holy Scriptures ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM_566.E2.80.937_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-CM_566.E2.80.937-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World253_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-World253-6">[6]</a></sup> Other, less plausible, interpretations of this passage have been suggested—for example that it means Bede stopped writing about scripture in his fifty-ninth year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whiting_4_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whiting_4-7">[7]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cuthbert is probably the same person as the later abbot of Monkwearmouth-Jarrow, but this is not entirely certain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reread9_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reread9-9">[8]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">Isidore of Seville</a> lists six orders below a deacon, but these orders need not have existed at Monkwearmouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World253_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-World253-6">[6]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The key phrase is <i>per linguae curationem</i>, which is variously translated as "how his tongue was healed", "[a] canker on the tongue", or, following a different interpretation of <i>curationem</i>, "the guidance of my tongue".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FC_W_21_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-FC_W_21-31">[28]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The letter itself is in <i>Bedae Opera de Temporibus</i> edited by C. W. Jones, pp. 307–315</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-112"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-112">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Laistner provides a list of works definitely or tentatively identified as in Bede's library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BLTW_L_2_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-BLTW_L_2-111">[106]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bede&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
